Translation Evaluation Packet

Language: Vietnamese

Reviewer: Vietnamese Reviewer

Total Items: 48

Date Generated: 2025-11-30

Instructions for Reviewers

Thank you for participating in this translation quality evaluation. Please follow these guidelines:

  1. Read carefully: Review the original English text, the translation, and the back-translation.
  2. Rate each criterion: Use the 1-5 scale for each quality dimension.
  3. Note critical errors: Flag any translation errors that could harm patients.
  4. Be objective: Evaluate based on accuracy, not personal preference.

Rating Scale

  • 5 - Excellent: Perfect or near-perfect quality
  • 4 - Good: Minor issues that don't affect meaning
  • 3 - Acceptable: Some issues but core meaning preserved
  • 2 - Poor: Significant issues affecting comprehension
  • 1 - Very Poor: Major errors or incomprehensible
SAFETY CRITICAL: If you find any translation that could lead to patient harm (wrong medication instructions, missing warnings, etc.), please mark it as a critical error and describe the issue in the notes section.

Evaluation Criteria Reference

CriterionDescription
Overall AccuracyHow accurately does the translation convey the original meaning?
Medical AccuracyAre medical terms and concepts translated correctly?
Cultural AppropriatenessIs the translation culturally appropriate for the target audience?
ClarityHow easy is the translation to understand for a native speaker?
Safety PreservationAre all warnings.and safety information preserved?
ActionabilityCan a patient follow the instructions based on this translation?

Evaluation Items

Item: EVAL_0007 | Document: CARD_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart can no longer pump oxygen-rich blood to supply the body effectively. When symptoms become severe, hospitalization may be necessary. This document talks about what you need to do to take care of yourself when leaving the hospital. When You Are in the Hospital You have been hospitalized to be treated for heart failure. Heart failure occurs when the heart muscle becomes weak or has difficulty relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that pushes fluid throughout the body. Like any pump, if the amount of blood pumped out is not enough, fluid will not circulate well and will back up in places it should not. In your body, this means fluid will build up in the lungs, abdomen, and legs. When you are in the hospital: The health care team has closely adjusted the amount of fluid you drink or receive through an intravenous (IV) line. They also monitored and measured the amount of urine you passed. You may have been given medicine to help your body get rid of excess fluid. You may have had tests done to check how your heart is working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will gradually recover. You may need help with taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Self-Monitoring at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale as soon as you wake up ‚Äì before eating but after using the bathroom. Make sure you wear similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Record your weight every day on a chart so you can keep track. During the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Am I more short of breath when doing daily activities? Do my clothes or shoes feel tighter? Are my ankles or legs swollen? Am I coughing more often? Does the cough sound wet? Do I have shortness of breath at night or when lying down? If you have new (or unusual) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat anything different from usual today or...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body effectively. When symptoms become severe, hospitalization may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when discharged from the hospital. When You Were In The Hospital You were hospitalized to treat heart failure. Heart failure occurs when your heart muscle is weak or has difficulty relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluid throughout your body. Like any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not sufficient, fluid does not move well and it backs up in places it should not be. In your body, this means fluid accumulates in the lungs, abdomen and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your healthcare team carefully adjusted the amount of fluid you drank or received through intravenous (IV) lines. They also monitored and measured the amount of urine you produced. You may have been given medication to help your body get rid of excess fluid. You may have had tests done to check how well your heart is working. What To Expect When You Go Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need someone to help take care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of this is normal. Self-Checking At Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you wake up -- before eating but after using the bathroom. Make sure you wear similar clothing each time you weigh. Record your weight every day on a chart so you can track it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Am I more short of breath when doing daily activities? Do my clothes or shoes feel tight? Are my ankles or legs swollen? Am I coughing more? Does my cough sound wet? Am I having trouble breathing at night or when lying down? If you have new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something unusual or try new foods? Did I take all my medications correctly at the ...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition where the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body effectively. When symptoms become severe, hospitalization may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when leaving the hospital. When You Were in the Hospital You were in the hospital to treat heart failure. Heart failure occurs when the heart muscle is weak or has trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluid (liquid) throughout the body. Just like any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluid will not move well and gets stuck in places it should not be. In your body, this means fluid builds up in the lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: * The health care team closely adjusted the amount of fluid you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also monitored and measured the amount of urine you put out. * You may have been given medicine to help the body get rid of excess fluid. * You may have had tests done to check how well the heart is working. Things to Know When at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when first coming home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Self-Check at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you wake up -- before eating but after using the bathroom. Make sure you wear the same clothes each time you weigh. Record the weight every day on a tracking sheet so you can track it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: * Is my energy level normal? * Am I more short of breath when doing daily activities? * Do my clothes or shoes feel tight? * Are my ankles or legs swollen? * Am I coughing more often? Does the cough sound like it has phlegm (wet)? * Do I have shortness of breath at night or when lying down? If you have new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: * Did I eat something unusual or try ...

Item: EVAL_0031 | Document: CARD_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form blood clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as instructed. If this happens, you may be more likely to form blood clots or have bleeding problems. Things to know when you go home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form blood clots. People who have new heart valves, enlarged hearts, heart rhythm disorders (abnormal heart rhythms), or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may bleed more easily, even when doing things you usually do. Changing how you take warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods can all change how warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form blood clots or have bleeding problems. Taking warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as instructed. Only take the exact dose that the health care provider prescribed. If you forget a dose, contact the provider for advice. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to support your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. Back-Translation (to English):

Using warfarin Warfarin is a type of medication that makes your blood less likely to clot. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as directed. Changing how you take warfarin, taking other medications at the same time or at different times, and eating certain foods can change how warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to get blood clots or have bleeding problems. Things to Know When at Home Warfarin is a type of medication that makes your blood less likely to clot. This can be important if: You have had a blood clot in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your doctor is concerned that blood clots may form in your body. People with new heart valves, enlarged heart, abnormal heart rhythm, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may bleed more easily, even from activities you do every day. Changing how you take warfarin, taking other medications, and eating certain foods can all change how warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to get blood clots or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as directed. Only take the dose your doctor has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your doctor for advice. If your pill looks different from the previous prescription, contact your doctor or pharmacist immediately. The pills have different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also printed on the pill. Your doctor will test your blood during regular check-ups. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. This test helps ensure you are taking the right amount of warfarin needed for your body. Alcohol and some medications can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol when you are taking warfarin. Talk to your doctor before taking any over-the-counter medications, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medications. Inform all of your doctors that you are taking warfarin. T...

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Original English:

Heart attack ‚Äì discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Do not change your exercise plan before talking with y...

Myocardial infarction ‚Äì discharge Myocardial infarction occurs when the blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that a part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This document talks about what you need to do to take care of yourself after leaving the hospital. When you are in the hospital You were in the hospital because you had a myocardial infarction. Myocardial infarction occurs when the blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that a part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What you will experience when you get home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as if you have to be very careful with everything you do. All of these feelings are normal. In most people, they will go away after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You need to know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel heaviness, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in the back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel nauseated. You may feel tired and short of breath, sweaty, dizzy, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, carrying things, having sex, or when you are outside in the cold. It can also occur when you are resting or may wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to handle it when you have chest pain. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Live gently for the first 4 to 6 weeks after a myocardial infarction. Avoid lifting heavy things. If possible, ask someone to help with housework. Every afternoon, rest for 30 to 60 minutes during the first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get enough sleep. Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Attack ‚Äì Discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that a part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after being discharged from the hospital. When You Were in the Hospital You were admitted to the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that a part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect When Going Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and like you have to be very careful with what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They will go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you are discharged to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in the back, shoulders, and abdominal area. You may have indigestion or feel nauseous. You may feel tired and short of breath, sweaty, dizzy, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting heavy objects, sexual activity, or when you are outside in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or may wake you when you are sleeping. Know how to handle chest pain when it occurs. Talk to your health care provider about what to do. Rest for the first 4 to 6 weeks after a heart attack. Avoid lifting heavy objects. Ask for help with housework if possible. Rest for 30 to 60 minutes in the afternoon during the first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get enough sleep. Before starting exercise, your doctor may ask you to do a stress test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you are discharged or soon after. Do not change your exercise plan before talking to your doctor. ...

Original English:

Heart attack ‚Äì discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Item: EVAL_0079 | Document: CARD_004 | Model: Model C

Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), or tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? Do they have any side effects? What...

Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Back-Translation (to English):

High blood pressure - things to ask the doctor When your heart pumps blood into the arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called blood pressure. Your blood pressure is expressed by two numbers: systolic blood pressure over diastolic blood pressure. Systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure level during the heartbeat cycle. Diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure level. When blood pressure goes up too high, it will cause extra pressure for the heart and blood vessels. If blood pressure is always at a high level, you will be at high risk of having a heart attack and other vascular diseases, stroke, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask the doctor or medical professional to help you take care of your blood pressure. The questions How can I change my lifestyle to lower blood pressure? * What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it okay to occasionally eat dishes that are not good for the heart? What are some ways to eat healthily when I go out to eat at a restaurant? * Do I need to limit the amount of salt intake? Are there other types of spices that I can use to make food more appetizing? * Is drinking alcohol okay? How much is okay? * What can I do to quit smoking? Is it okay to be near people who are smoking? Should I measure blood pressure at home? * Which type of monitor should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? * How often do I need to measure blood pressure? Type 2 diabetes - things to ask your doctor Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes high sugar (glucose) levels in your blood. The disease can damage organs in the body. The disease can also lead to heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control symptoms, prevent damage due to diabetes, and improve your life. Below are questions you may want to ask the doctor or medical staff to help you care for your diabetes. Questions Ask the doctor to check the nerves, skin, and pulse in your feet. Also, ask the following questions: * How often should I check my feet? What should I do when checking my feet? What problems should I call the doctor about? * Who should cut my toenails? Is it okay if I cut my toenails myself? * How should I care for my feet daily? What kind of shoes and socks should I wear? * Should I go see a foot specialist (podiatrist)? Ask the doctor about exercising, including: * Before starting, do I need to check my heart? Check my eyes? Check my feet? * What exercise program should I follow? What types of activities should I avoid? * When exercising, when should I check blood sugar? What should I bring along when exercising? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust medication when exercising? When should I go for the next eye exam with an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist)? What eye problems should I contact the eye doctor about? Ask the doctor about seeing a nutrition expert. Questions for the nutrition expert may include: * Which foods increase my blood sugar the most? * Which foods can help me reach weight loss goals? Ask the doctor about your diabetes medications: * When should I take the medicine? * What should I do if I forget to take a dose? * Are there any side effects? How often should I check blood sugar levels at home? Should I check at different times of the day? What level is too low? What level is too high? What should...

Item: EVAL_0127 | Document: DIAB_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking your insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insul...

Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent it. What is low blood sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can be harmful to you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a reason to take action immediately. Recognizing low blood sugar Know how to recognize when your blood sugar is dropping. Symptoms include: Weak or feeling tired Shaky Sweating Headache Hungry Feeling restless, worried, or uneasy Easily irritable Hard to think clearly Double vision or blurred vision Fast heartbeat or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar can be too low even when you do not have symptoms. Back-Translation (to English):

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar can occur in people with diabetes who are using insulin or some other medications to control diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What Is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can be harmful to you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for hypoglycemia if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medications: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at high risk for hypoglycemia if you have had hypoglycemia before. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to recognize when your blood sugar is dropping. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling restless, anxious, or nervous Feeling irritable Difficulty thinking clearly Double vision or blurred vision Fast heartbeat or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar can be too low even when you don't have symptoms. If it goes too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people with long-standing diabetes no longer have the ability to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemia unawareness. Ask your health care provider whether wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when blood sugar is getting too low to help prevent symptoms. Checking Blood Sugar Regularly Talk with your doctor about when you should check your blood sugar each day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of hypoglycemia are: Taking insulin or diabetes medication at the wrong time Taking too much insulin or diabet...

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. Hypoglycemia - self-care Hypoglycemia is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) level is lower than normal. Symptoms include: * Weak or feeling tired * Shaking * Sweating * Headache * Hunger * Feeling uneasy, anxious, or nervous * Feeling irritable * Difficulty thinking clearly * Double vision (seeing one as two) or blurry vision * Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar can be too low even when you do not have symptoms. Item: EVAL_0151 | Document: RESP_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Asthma - quick-relief medications Quick-relief asthma medications work quickly to control asthma symptoms. You use them when you have coughing, wheezing, difficulty breathing, or when you have an asthma attack. These medications are also called rescue medications. Many medications in this group are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of the airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medications that are right for you. This plan will include when you should take your medications and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out of medication. Bring enough medication when you travel. Short-acting beta agonists Short-acting beta agonists are the most common quick-relief medications for treating asthma attacks and are considered bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercise to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing your airway muscles, and this helps you breathe more easily during an asthma attack. Let your health care provider know if you are using quick-relief medications two times a week or more to control asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be well controlled, and your health care provider may need to change your daily controller medication dose. Some quick-relief asthma medications include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta agonists can cause the following side effects: Anxiety. Original English:

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. While there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to regulate your symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent getting a lung infection? How can I get help quitting smoking? Will fumes, dust, or having pets make my COPD worse? What are some signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should contact my provider? What should I do when I feel I am not breathing well enough? Am I taking my COPD medicines the right way? What medicines should I be taking every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What are some exercises I can do to keep my muscles strong, even if I cannot walk around very much? Should I consider pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I save some of my energy around the house...

Although there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to control the symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? What kinds of medicines should I take every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I forget a day or a dose? What kinds of medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called rescue medicines or emergency medicines)? Is it okay if I use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? With which side effects should I contact my health care provider? Am I using the inhaler correctly? Should I use a spacer? How will I know when my inhaler is running out of medicine? When should I use a nebulizer and when should I use an inhaler? What should I change in my home? What do I need to change at my workplace? What shots or vaccines do I need? Do I need oxygen? If so, do I need to use it all the time? Are there any changes in diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Do I need oxygen on the airplane? What about at the airport? What kinds of medicines should I bring with me? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? Item: EVAL_0199 | Document: MED_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

ACE Inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They are used to treat problems of the heart, blood vessels, and kidneys. Benefits of ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines help your heart not have to work too hard by lowering blood pressure. This helps some types of heart disease not become more severe. Most people with heart failure use one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, stroke, or myocardial infarction (heart attack). They can help reduce the risk of stroke or myocardial infarction. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from becoming more severely damaged, or even improve kidney function, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider whether you should use these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most of them are about equally effective. Side effects may differ between medicines. How to use ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills taken by mouth. Take all medicines exactly as your health care provider has instructed you. Follow up with your provider regularly. The provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicine is working properly. The provider may change your dose over time. Back-Translation (to English):

ACE Inhibitors (ACE) Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are a type of medication. This medication treats problems regarding the heart, blood vessels, and kidneys. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work more gently by lowering blood pressure. This helps prevent certain types of heart disease from getting worse. Most people with heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They can help reduce the risk of having a stroke or heart attack. The medicine is also used to treat kidney problems. This can help your kidneys not get worse or even improve kidney function, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask the doctor if you should take these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most types work equally well. Side effects may vary depending on the type. How to take ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills taken by mouth. Take all medicines exactly as directed by the doctor. Follow up regularly with the doctor. The doctor will check blood pressure and do blood tests to ensure the medicine is working effectively. The doctor may change your dosage from time to time. Also: * Try to take the medicine at the same time every day. * Do not stop taking the medicine without consulting the doctor first. * Plan ahead so as not to run out of medicine. Make sure that you bring enough medicine when traveling. * Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to the doctor. * Let the doctor know what other medicines you are taking, including any you buy without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium supplements, or herbs or dietary supplements. * Do not take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Item: EVAL_0223 | Document: EMER_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home follow your health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You're in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs heal. After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing easy tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Paying attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Talking or understanding others Thinking Seeing to one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may have physical or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use. Family and caregivers may need to help with: Exercises to keep your elbows, shoulders, and other joints loose Watching for joint tightening (contractures) Making sure splints are used in the correct way Making sure arms and legs are in a good position when sitting or lying If you or your loved one is using a wheelchair, fol...

It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use. Family and caregivers may need to help with: Exercises to keep your elbows, shoulders, and other joints loose Watching for joint tightening (contractures) Making sure splints are used in the correct way Making sure arms and legs are in a good position when sitting or lying If you or your loved one is using a wheelchair, fol...

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to a part of the brain is stopped. At home, follow the health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You Were In The Hospital First, you were treated to prevent further damage to the brain, and help the heart, lungs, and other vital organs recover. After the condition stabilized, health care providers did tests and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent future strokes. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. Things To Know When At Home Because the brain may be damaged from the stroke, you may notice problems with: * Behavior changes * Doing simple tasks * Memory * Moving one side of the body * Muscle spasms * Attention * Sensation or awareness of a body part * Swallowing * Speaking or understanding others * Thinking * Seeing to one side (hemianopia) You may need help with daily activities that you used to do alone before. You may need physical therapy or occupational therapy to restore the ability to perform these functions. Depression after stroke is quite common as you learn to live with the changes. This condition can develop right after the stroke or up to 2 years later. Do not drive without the permission of the health care provider. Ask the health care provider, therapist, or nurse about changes in the home to help make daily activities easier. Find out what you can do to prevent falls and keep the bathroom safe when using it. Item: EVAL_0247 | Document: SURG_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Back-Translation (to English):

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision opens up. This can happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with stitches. What To Know When At Home If the surgeon does not close your wound with stitches, you need to care for it at home because it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom up. The dressing helps absorb drainage and prevents the skin from closing before the wound underneath is filled in. Proper Hand Washing It is important to clean your hands before changing the dressing. You can use an alcohol-based hand sanitizer. Or you can wash your hands following these steps: Remove all jewelry from your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet" song once). Clean under your fingernails as well. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change the dressing. To prepare to change the dressing: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all necessary supplies ready. Prepare a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Gently peel the tape from the skin. Use clean medical gloves (do not need to be sterile) to hold the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your health care provider instructs you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after removing the old dressing. Wound Care You can use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around the wound: Use normal saline solution or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline or soapy water,...

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Incision care - open wound An incision is a cut through the skin made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery you had done. Sometimes, the incision opens up. This can happen along the entire cut or just a part. Your surgeon may decide not to stitch the wound closed with sutures. Things to Know at Home If the surgeon does not stitch the wound closed, you need to care for it at home, because it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom up. Gauze dressing helps absorb drainage and keeps the skin from closing before the part underneath the wound is filled in. Proper Hand Washing Washing hands clean before changing the dressing is very important. You can use an alcohol-based hand sanitizer. Or, you can wash your hands following these steps: 1. Remove all jewelry from hands. 2. Wet hands, pointing hands down under running warm water. 3. Add soap and scrub hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing the "Happy Birthday" or "Alphabet Song" one time). Clean under fingernails as well. 4. Rinse well. 5. Dry with a clean towel. Removing Old Dressing The doctor or medical staff will let you know how often to change the dressing. To prepare to change the dressing: * Wash hands clean before touching the dressing. * Ensure you have all necessary supplies ready. * Have a clean work surface. Remove old dressing: * Carefully loosen the adhesive tape from the skin. * Use clean medical gloves (sterile not needed) to hold the old dressing and pull it off. * If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless the doctor instructs you to pull it off when dry. * Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. * Wash hands clean again after removing the old dressing. Item: EVAL_0271 | Document: SURG_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Hip Replacement - Discharge You have had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This document guides you on how to care for your new hip after leaving the hospital. When You Are Still in the Hospital You have had hip replacement surgery to replace all or part of the hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect When You Get Home When you get home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without much help. Most people no longer need to use them after 2 to 4 weeks. Ask your surgeon when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with very little help and be able to get in and out of bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to go to the bathroom by yourself without much help. You will need to be careful not to dislocate the artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises to help make the new hip stronger and follow special precautions. You will need to have someone at home with you for 1 to 2 weeks after leaving the hospital or rehabilitation center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you may return to your previous level of activity. You will need to avoid certain sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you can still take part in low-impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and playing golf. Arranging Your Home Your bed should be low enough so that your feet touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. The bed should also be high enough so that when you sit on the edge, your hips are higher than your knees. You may not need a hospital bed, but the mattress should be firm, not too soft. Keep your home free of items that can easily cause tripping. Learn how to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or ...

Hip replacement - discharge You have undergone surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what to do to care for your new hip when leaving the hospital. **When You are at the Hospital** You had hip replacement surgery to replace all or part of the hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. **Things to Note at Home** When going home, you will be able to walk with a walker or crutches without much help. Most people do not need to use them after 2 to 4 weeks. Ask the surgeon when to stop using crutches. You will also be able to dress yourself with little help and can get in and out of bed or a chair on your own. You will also be able to go to the toilet without much help. You need to be careful not to dislocate the artificial hip, especially during the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises to help the new hip get stronger and take special precautions. You will need someone to stay with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after leaving the hospital or rehabilitation center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you will be able to return to your previous level of activity. You will need to avoid certain sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like rugby and soccer. But you will be able to do gentle activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and playing golf. **Arranging the Home** Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when sitting at the edge of the bed. The bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when sitting at the edge of the bed. You may not need a hospital bed, but the mattress must be firm. Item: EVAL_0291 | Document: CARD_001 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition where the heart no longer has the ability to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body effectively. When symptoms become serious, you may need to be hospitalized. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when leaving the hospital. When You Are In The Hospital You have been hospitalized to treat heart failure. Heart failure occurs when your heart muscle is weak or has difficulty relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluid in the body. Like any pump, if the flow out of the pump is insufficient, fluid will not move well and gets stuck in places where it shouldn't be. In your body, this means fluid accumulates in the lungs, abdomen and legs. While you are in the hospital: Your health care team has tightly adjusted the amount of fluid you drink or receive through intravenous (IV) line. They also monitored and measured the amount of urine you produced. You may have been given medication to help the body eliminate excess fluid. You may have done tests to check the level of heart activity. What To Expect At Home Your energy will gradually recover. You may need help in taking care of yourself when newly home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these are normal. Self-Check At Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you wake up - before eating but after using the bathroom. Make sure you wear similar clothes each time you weigh. Record your weight every day on a chart so you can track. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I have more difficulty breathing when doing daily activities? Do my clothes or shoes feel tight? Are my ankles or legs swollen? Am I coughing more frequently? Does my cough sound wet? Do I have difficulty breathing at night or when lying down? If you have new (or unusual) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat anything out of the ordinary or try a new food? Did I take all medication correctly and on time? Die...

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Using warfarin Warfarin is a type of medication that reduces your blood's ability to clot. It is very important that you must take warfarin exactly as directed. Changing the way you take warfarin, taking other medications together with it or at different times, and eating certain types of foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may have more likelihood of forming blood clots or encountering bleeding problems. Things to know when at home Warfarin is a type of medication that reduces your blood's ability to clot. This may be important if: - You have ever had a blood clot in the leg, arm, heart or brain. - Your doctor is concerned that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, enlarged heart, abnormal heart rhythm or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more prone to bleeding, even from activities you normally do. Changing the way you take warfarin, taking other medications and eating certain types of foods can all change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may have more likelihood of forming blood clots or encountering bleeding problems. How to take warfarin It is very important that you must take warfarin exactly as directed. - Only take the exact dosage your doctor has prescribed. If you forget a dose, please contact your doctor for advice. - If your medication looks different from the previous prescription, please contact your doctor or pharmacist immediately. The tablets have different colors, depending on the dosage. The dosage is also written on the tablet. - Your doctor will test your blood during regular check-ups. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. This test helps ensure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help the body.
